The Core Problem: Form Entering Matter
Initial formulation of the specification problem and first objection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the entry of form into matter leads to the problem of specification without a specifier, and why appealing to a specific form does not resolve it.

Core Idea
Specification demands a specifier; form cannot pick its own location.

This passage opens with a classic kalam argument: when a form enters matter, it cannot specify its own location without a specifier. An objector suggests that a specific form (ṣūra nawʿiyya) might do the job, but the response shows that simply shifts the problem. We'll follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Note the question and answer structure.
Look for the phrase 'تخصيص بلا مخصص' – specification without a specifier.
2
Identify the initial problem
The passage begins with the claim that if a form enters matter, it must either be in all locations (impossible) or be specified. The specification requires a specifier.
The author presents a dilemma.
3
Analyze the objection and response
The objector says a specific form could necessitate a location. The response is to transfer the discussion to that specific form itself: how does it get specified?
This shifts the problem without solving it.
4
Reflect on the example
The author uses the example of air becoming water then earth to show how a prior form could determine location, but this still relies on a prior specifier.
The example illustrates the need for a chain of specification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعند حلول الصورة إما أن تكون في جميع الأحياز وهو تخصيص بلا مخصص ، لأن نسبة الصورة الجسمية إلى جميع الأحياز على السوية . فإن قيل : لعل معها صورة نوعية تقتضي الاختصاص. قلنا: فننقل الكلام إلى خصوصية ذلك المظهر أعني الصورة النوعية دون سائر المظاهر ، ثم تعيين هذا الحيز من بين الأحياز التي هي أجزاء حيز كلية ذلك النوع ، ولا يرد النقض بهذا من الأرض مثلاً ، حيث يخصص بهذا الجزء من حيز الأرض لجواز أن يكون ذلك بسبب صورة سابقة مقتضية لهذا الوضع المقتضي بهذا الحيز كما إذا صار جزء من الهواء ماء ثم صار أرضاً فإنه ينزل على استقامة إلى أن يقع في حيز من الماء ثم الأرض. وسيجيء لهذا زيادة بيان ، وكذا الكلام في وجه اختصاص المادة بما لها من الصورة النوعية على ما نفصله في الفرع الخامس . يرد النقض به نعم يتوجه أن يقال لم لا يجوز أن تكون للهيولي المجردة أوصاف وأحوال غير الصورة والأوضاع تعدها للاختصاص عند التجسم ببعض الأوضاع والأحياز على التعيين، وأما الرفع بإسناد الاختصاص إلى القادر المختار على ما ذكره الإمام فلا يتأتى على أصول القائلين بالهيولي .
When the form enters [matter], either it is in all locations — and that is specification without a specifier, because the relation of the bodily form to all locations is equal. If it is said: perhaps with it there is a specific form (ṣūra nawʿiyya) that necessitates specification. We say: then we transfer the discussion to the particularity of that manifestation, namely the specific form, excluding other manifestations; then the determination of this location among the locations that are parts of the total space of that species. And the refutation by this is not rebutted by the example of the earth, where it is specified to this part of the space of the earth, because it is possible that this is due to a prior form necessitating this position which necessitates this location, as when a part of air becomes water then becomes earth, it descends straight until it falls into a location of water then earth. A fuller explanation will come for this. And similarly the discussion concerning the specification of matter with its specific form as we detail in the fifth branch. The refutation by this is rebutted; indeed it is directed that one say: why is it not permissible that pure matter (al-hayūlā al-mujarrada) has attributes and states other than the form and positions that prepare it for specification when it becomes embodied (al-tajassum) in certain positions and locations determinately? As for removing [the problem] by attributing specification to the free agent (al-qādir al-mukhtār) as mentioned by the Imam, it does not hold on the principles of those who affirm matter (al-qāʾilīn bi-l-hayūlā).
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage discusses a problem faced by those who affirm prime matter (hayūlā) when a form is said to 'enter' it. The author argues that if a form were to enter matter, it would have to occupy a specific location; otherwise it would be in all locations equally, which is specification without a specifier (takhṣīṣ bilā mukhaṣṣiṣ). An objector suggests that a specific form (ṣūra nawʿiyya) might determine a particular location. The response shifts the problem to that specific form and its determination within the space of its genus. The author uses the example of air becoming water then earth to show how a prior form could necessitate a location. A further objection proposes that pure matter might have preparatory attributes that determine location. The author concludes that appealing to a free agent (qādir mukhtār) does not work on the principles of those who affirm matter, because such an appeal presupposes a different ontology. This passage is a refutation of a potential answer to the problem of specification.

Key Terms
صورة
هيولي
تخصيص
حلول
حيز
صورة نوعية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That a specific form (ṣūra nawʿiyya) can determine its own location without external cause.
Correction: The specific form itself requires a specifier; otherwise the problem recurs.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main problem with the idea that a specific form (ṣūra nawʿiyya) determines its own location?
A) Specific forms do not exist.
B) The specific form itself must be specified to a particular location.
C) All forms are equally related to all locations.
D) The free agent is the only possible specifier.

Key Takeaway
The initial problem of form specification in matter cannot be resolved by appealing to a specific form, because that form itself requires a specifier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the form requires an external specifier to determine its location, what does that imply about the patterns and circumstances of your life? Who or what is specifying the 'place' you find yourself in right now? Can you truly claim self-determination, or is there a specifier beyond your control?
?
The objection suggests a specific form might explain its own location, but the response shows that this merely pushes the question back. In your own life, when you attribute something to your own nature or choices, have you considered that even those may be specified by a deeper cause? Where does the chain end?

Further Objections and the Free Agent Solution
Further objections and rejection of free agent solution

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why attributing specification to a free agent is inconsistent with the principles of those who affirm prime matter (hayūlā).

Core Idea
Appealing to the free agent only works if you reject prime matter.

After the initial exchange, the passage raises two more points: an objection that pure matter might have preparatory attributes for specification, and the claim that the free agent (al-qādir al-mukhtār) could directly specify. The author shows that both fail within the framework of those who affirm matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the latter part of the source. Notice the structure: an objection followed by a rebuttal.
The key phrase is 'أما الرفع بإسناد الاختصاص إلى القادر المختار فلا يتأتى على أصول القائلين بالهيولي'.
2
Understand the objection about matter's attributes
The objector suggests that prime matter (hayūlā mujarrada) might have attributes that prepare it for a specific location when it becomes embodied.
This is an attempt to find a natural explanation within matter itself.
3
Analyze the rejection of the free agent solution
The author says that attributing specification to a free agent does not work on the principles of those who affirm matter. Why? Because such an appeal assumes matter is passive and form is imposed – but the free agent would be superfluous if matter has its own attributes.
The inconsistency lies in combining prime matter (which is supposed to be unqualified) with direct divine action.
4
Reflect on the overall argument
Consider why the passage ends with this conclusion. It shows that the problem of specification is a serious weakness for the Ashʿarī occasionalist position that affirms prime matter.
This argument is part of a broader critique of the use of hayūlā in kalam.

First Answer: Disconnection or Passivity
The first answer to the objection: either disconnection or passivity necessitates matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first answer to the objection about matter, which states that either disconnection (infisal) or passivity (infi'al) necessitates matter, and the clarification of a misinterpretation from Sharh al-Isharat.

Core Idea
Matter is necessitated by either disconnection or passivity in bodies.

In responding to an objection against the necessity of matter, the author presents two answers. The first answer points out that what is required is not a single property but one of two: either disconnection or passivity. Both, as earlier proofs showed, entail matter. This lesson unpacks that answer and explains a subtle point regarding a passage from Sharh al-Isharat.

Guided Path
1
Read the first answer
Read the Arabic passage for the first answer and its translation. Notice how the author lists two possibilities: disconnection and passivity, with examples.
2
Identify the key terms
Focus on the terms انفصال (disconnection) and انفعال (passivity). Understand why each necessitates matter according to earlier proofs.
The author refers to 'the two proofs of disconnection and passivity' from earlier in the text.
3
Examine the clarification
The author warns against misinterpreting a statement from Sharh al-Isharat. Read the explanation to see how passivity alone suffices even if disconnection is not present.
This avoids the redundancy of requiring both.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب عن الأول بوجهين : أحدهما : أن المراد لزوم أحد الأمرين أعني الانفصال كما في تشكلات الماء تجعله مياها أو مجرد الانفعال كما في الشمع ، وكل منهما يستلزم المادة على ما سبق من برهاني الانفصال والانفعال مع ما عليهما من الإشكال. ولا خفاء في أن هذا مع كونه مخالفاً لظاهر تقرير القوم مشتمل على استدراك، لأن إمكان الانفعال لازم قطعاً، فلا معنى لضم الانفصال إليه، وجعل اللازم أحدهما ، ولا ينبغي أن يحمل على هذا المعنى عبارة شرح الإشارات (۱) . حيث قال : هذا الاعتراض ليس بقادح في الفرض، لأنا لم نجعل لزوم المحال مقصوراً على لزوم الفصل والوصل، بل عليه وعلى لزوم الانفعال) ، معناها أنا رتبنا لزوم المحال على لزوم الانفصال، ولزوم الانفعال جميعاً ، فإن ثبت كلا اللزومين فذاك ، وإلا فلا خفاء في لزوم الانفعال وهو كاف في لزوم المادة . وثانيهما: أن ليس المراد انفصال الجسم في نفسه، بل انفصال الأجسام بعضها عن بعض لمعنى عدم الاتصال عما من شأنه الاتصال، فإن هذا المحوج إلى المادة لا مجرد التمايز والافتراق.

وللتنبيه على هذا المعنى تعرضوا مع الانفصال للاتصال، وإلا فلا دخل لاتصال الأجسام بعضها ببعض في اختلاف أشكالها ومقاديرها، وعلى هذا يحمل ما قال في شرح الإشارات: أن المغايرة بين الأجسام لا تتصور إلا بانفصال بعضها عن بعض، واتصال بعضها ببعض، وذلك مستلزم للمادة ، ولما لاح على هذا الطريق أثر الضعف بناء على أنهم بنوا ثبوت المادة على إمكان الاتصال والانفصال في الجسم نفسه، حتى لو لم يوجد إلا كذلك لا الاتصال والانفصال فيما بين الأجسام، وأن دعوى إمكان الاتصال فيما بين كل جسمين حتى الفلك والعنصر بحسب الطبيعة الجسمية ربما لا عدل إلى طريق الانفعال فقال : وبالجملة لا يمكن أن يحصل الاختلافات المقدارية والشكلية عن فاعلها في الامتداد، إلا بعد أن يكون فيه قوة الانفصال المقتضية للمادة.
And it was answered about the first with two aspects: First: that what is meant is the necessity of one of two matters, namely disconnection (al-infisal) as in the formations of water making it waters, or mere passivity (al-infi'al) as in wax, and each of them necessitates matter (al-madda) according to what preceded from the two proofs of disconnection and passivity along with the difficulties they entail. And it is not hidden that this, while contradicting the apparent formulation of the [scholars], contains a supplementation, because the possibility of passivity is necessarily required, so there is no meaning to adding disconnection to it and making the necessary one of them, and the expression of Sharh al-Isharat (1) should not be interpreted in this sense. Where he said: 'This objection is not damaging to the hypothesis, because we did not confine the necessity of impossibility to the necessity of separation and conjunction, but rather to it and to the necessity of passivity,' its meaning is that we arranged the necessity of impossibility upon the necessity of disconnection and the necessity of passivity together. So if both necessities are established, then that is it; otherwise, there is no hiddenness in the necessity of passivity, and it suffices for the necessity of matter. Second: that what is meant is not the disconnection of the body in itself, but rather the disconnection of bodies from each other in the sense of the absence of contact from what has the nature of contact, for this is what necessitates matter, not mere differentiation and separation.

And to draw attention to this meaning, they discussed connection (al-ittisal) along with disconnection, otherwise there would be no role for the connection of bodies with each other in the difference of their shapes and magnitudes, and upon this is interpreted what was said in Sharh al-Isharat: that the difference between bodies is not conceivable except by the disconnection of some from others and the connection of some with others, and that necessitates matter. And when weakness appeared on this path due to the fact that they based the establishment of matter on the possibility of connection and disconnection in the body itself, even if it existed only in that way without connection and disconnection between bodies, and that the claim of the possibility of connection between every two bodies, even the celestial and the elemental, according to bodily nature perhaps... he turned to the path of passivity and said: 'In summary, the quantitative and formal differences cannot occur from their agent in the extension unless there is in it the power of disconnection which necessitates matter.'
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage is an answer to a prior objection (not fully stated here) concerning the necessity of matter for bodies. The author gives two aspects (وجهان) of the answer. The first aspect clarifies that the required property is either disconnection (انفصال) or passivity (انفعال), using examples: water formations exhibit disconnection (waters separating), wax exhibits passivity (being shaped). Both entail matter, as earlier proofs of disconnection and passivity showed. The author then notes a potential misinterpretation of a passage from Sharh al-Isharat, explaining that the intended meaning is that impossibility arises from both disconnection and passivity together, but passivity alone suffices. The second aspect redefines disconnection: it is not a body's internal separation, but the separation of bodies from each other (lack of contact). This, not mere numerical difference, necessitates matter. The author then elaborates that this understanding is why earlier scholars mentioned connection alongside disconnection: without inter-body connection/disconnection, shape and magnitude differences would not require matter. He notes a weakness in this approach because the proof originally relied on connection/disconnection within one body, not between bodies. Therefore, an alternative path is introduced, focusing on the power of disconnection within the body to account for quantitative and formal differences, which ultimately requires matter.

Key Terms
انفصال
انفعال
مادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first answer, what does the necessity of matter depend on?
A) Only disconnection
B) Only passivity
C) Either disconnection or passivity
D) Both disconnection and passivity simultaneously

Key Takeaway
The first answer shows that matter is necessitated by either disconnection or passivity, and passivity alone is sufficient.

Second Answer: Inter-Body Disconnection and Shift to Passivity
The second answer: disconnection as inter-body separation, and the shift to passivity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second answer to the objection, which redefines disconnection as inter-body separation, and the subsequent shift to a passivity-based argument due to weaknesses in the inter-body approach.

Core Idea
Disconnection between bodies, not within a body, necessitates matter; but this leads to a weaker argument, prompting a turn to passivity.

The second answer to the objection offers a different interpretation: disconnection means bodies being separate from each other, not internal division. This interpretation explains why scholars discussed connection alongside disconnection. However, this path has a weakness because the original proofs assumed connection/disconnection within a single body. Consequently, the argument shifts to passivity, which more robustly necessitates matter. This lesson explores these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Read the second answer
Read the Arabic for the second answer and its translation. Note the redefinition of disconnection as between bodies.
2
Understand the role of connection
The author explains that scholars mentioned connection (ittisal) together with disconnection to highlight that inter-body connection/disconnection is what matters. Ask why this is necessary.
Without inter-body relation, shape differences would not require matter.
3
Follow the weakness and shift
The author notes a weakness: the original proof relied on connection/disconnection within one body. Then he presents an alternative: passivity within the body (power of disconnection) is necessary for quantitative and formal differences, which requires matter.
This shift avoids the earlier problem.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If matter is necessary for bodies to be differentiated, then your body is fundamentally temporary and dependent. How does that reality affect what you truly value—your physical appearance, your possessions, or something else? Write down one attachment you need to loosen today.

Second Objection on Simple Bodies and Its Two Answers
Author states objection and gives two answers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second objection against the need for matter in simple bodies and the two arguments (based on receptacle and preventer) used to answer it.

Core Idea
Simple bodies challenge matter theory; the answers save it by distinguishing receptacle and part-ness.

This passage tackles a subtle objection: how can part and whole share a single nature yet differ in shape and size? The author presents two nuanced answers that defend the role of matter in explaining such differences. You'll see how kalam arguments preserve consistency even in tricky cases.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objection uses simple bodies (celestial and elemental) where whole and part have the same nature but different shape and magnitude. This challenges the need for matter.
2
Follow the first answer (receptacle)
The first answer says matter receives both connection and disconnection, so differences in shape and magnitude are due to the receptacle, not the form.
3
Follow the second answer (preventer)
The second answer invokes the concept of a preventer: part-ness itself prevents the part from having the whole's shape/magnitude.
4
Notice the nuance about shape vs. magnitude
The passage clarifies that the objection primarily concerns shape, not magnitude; magnitude is incidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المستدركة في غاية الخفاء كتناهي الأبعاد . وثانيهما: النقض بكل بسيط من الفلكيات والعنصريات حيث كانت طبيعة الكل والجزء واحدة، مع أن الجزء ليس على شكل الكل ومقداره.

وأجيب عن النقض أيضاً بوجهين : أحدهما: أن هناك مادة تقبل الكلية والجزئية لقبولها بذاتها الاتصال والانفصال، فيعود اختلاف الشكل والمقدار فيما بين الكل والجزء إلى اختلاف القابل، وإن كان الفاعل واحداً هو الصورة النوعية ، بخلاف الصورة الجسمية . إذا فرضناها مجردة عن المادة، فإنه لا يتصور فيها ذلك، لأن حصول الجزئية بالانقسام، والكلية بالالتئام من لواحق المادة . وثانيهما: أن هناك مانعاً هو الجزئية، فإنه لما حصل للكل ذلك الشكل والمقدار امتنع بالضرورة حصوله للجزء، ما دام الجزء جزءاً ، والكل كلا ، ولا يتصور ذلك في الصورة المجردة من في الصورة المجردة من المادة وهذا عائد إلى الأول، إلا أنه ، يرد عليه أن الجزء وإن امتنع كونه على مقدار الكل، لا يمتنع كونه على شكله كتدوير الفلك وحامله ، والمقصود بالنقض هو الشكل، وإنما المقدار استطراد.
The additional [point] is in extreme subtlety, like the finitude of dimensions. And the second: the refutation by every simple [body] from the celestial and elemental things, where the nature of the whole and the part is one, whereas the part is not on the shape and magnitude of the whole.

And it was answered about the refutation also with two aspects: First: that there is a matter that accepts whole-ness and part-ness because it inherently accepts connection and disconnection, so the difference of shape and magnitude between the whole and the part returns to the difference of the receptacle, even if the agent is one, namely the specific form, unlike the bodily form. If we suppose it abstracted from matter, it is not conceivable in it, because the acquisition of part-ness by division and whole-ness by joining are accidents of matter. Second: that there is a preventer which is part-ness, because when the whole acquired that shape and magnitude, it is necessarily impossible for the part to acquire it as long as the part is a part and the whole is a whole, and that is not conceivable in the form abstracted from matter, and this returns to the first, except that it is objected that the part, although it is impossible for it to be on the magnitude of the whole, it is not impossible for it to be on its shape, like the rounding of the celestial sphere and its carrier, and the intended by the refutation is shape, while magnitude is incidental.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage first states the second objection (النقض) against a proof for matter. The objection targets simple bodies (celestial and elemental) where the whole and part share the same nature, yet the part does not have the same shape and magnitude as the whole. This challenges the need for matter. The author then provides two answers. The first answer (أحدهما) posits a matter that accepts both whole-ness and part-ness because it inherently receives connection and disconnection; thus the difference in shape and magnitude between whole and part is due to the difference in the receptacle (the matter), even if the agent (specific form) is one. In contrast, a form abstracted from matter cannot account for this, as part-ness from division and whole-ness from joining are properties that belong only to matter. The second answer (ثانيهما) invokes a preventer (مانع) which is part-ness itself: once the whole has a certain shape and magnitude, it is necessarily impossible for the part to have the same, as long as it remains a part. This preventer does not exist in a form abstracted from matter. However, the author notes an objection: the part could still have the same shape (e.g., a sphere's curvature) even if not the same magnitude. The reply clarifies that the intended point of the original refutation (النقض) is shape, not magnitude; magnitude is incidental.

Key Terms
بسيط
كل
جزء
شكل
مقدار
مادة
صورة نوعية
صورة جسمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The objection requires that the part differ from the whole in both shape and magnitude.
Correction: The objection's intended point is the difference in shape; magnitude is incidental.

Checkpoint
According to the first answer to the second objection, why does the part differ in shape and magnitude from the whole?
A) Because the agent is different
B) Because the receptacle (matter) differs
C) Because part-ness prevents it
D) Because the form is abstract

Key Takeaway
The second objection against matter from simple bodies is answered by showing that differences in shape between whole and part arise from matter's receptivity or from part-ness itself, not from a problem with the concept of form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world, do you instinctively assume things are simple (one nature) when they are actually compound? Where in your life do you mistake a part for the whole, or expect the part to mirror the whole exactly? How does this distort your understanding of God's creation?
?
The objector missed the role of matter because he focused only on form. In your own thinking, do you tend to ignore the 'material' or underlying realities—like context, history, or hidden causes—and only judge by visible appearances? What is one situation today where you need to look for the 'matter' behind the form?
?
The lesson shows that a part cannot have the whole's magnitude because of part-ness itself. Is there an area of your life where you are trying to be more than what your role or capacity allows—refusing to accept the limits of being a 'part'? What would it mean to humbly accept your part-ness in God's order?

Causality and Created Things
intermediate
5 min
The Weakness of the Connection/Separation Argument and the Shift to Passion
Critique of connection/separation argument and proposal of passion argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the argument from connection and separation for matter is considered weak and how the argument from passion (infi‘al) offers a stronger foundation.

Core Idea
Connection and separation between bodies cannot prove matter, but the potential for separation within a body can.

The author examines a common kalam proof for the existence of matter (al-madda) based on the observable connection and separation of bodies. However, he points out a subtle flaw: this proof relies on connections between bodies, not within the same body. To salvage the proof, the discussion shifts to the concept of passion (infi‘al), which points to an inherent potential for separation within extended substances.

Guided Path
1
Read and identify the two arguments
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Identify the first argument from connection/separation and the second argument from passion.
The passage is dense, so read slowly.
2
Understand the weakness of the first argument
Notice that the first argument bases matter on connection/separation between bodies, but the author shows it should be within the body itself. The text says this is weak because they built the existence of matter on possibility of connection/separation in the body itself, even if no such relation between bodies.
The weakness is that the argument assumes connection/separation within the same body, but they only observe it between different bodies.
3
See how the passion argument resolves it
The shift to passion (qawwat al-infisal) grounds matter in the body's own capacity for change. The author concludes that extension must have the potential for separation, which necessitates a substrate (matter).
This avoids the earlier problem because it focuses on internal potential, not external relations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللتنبيه على هذا المعنى تعرضوا مع الانفصال للاتصال، وإلا فلا دخل لاتصال الأجسام بعضها ببعض في اختلاف أشكالها ومقاديرها، وعلى هذا يحمل ما قال في شرح الإشارات: أن المغايرة بين الأجسام لا تتصور إلا بانفصال بعضها عن بعض، واتصال بعضها ببعض، وذلك مستلزم للمادة ، ولما لاح على هذا الطريق أثر الضعف بناء على أنهم بنوا ثبوت المادة على إمكان الاتصال والانفصال في الجسم نفسه، حتى لو لم يوجد إلا كذلك لا الاتصال والانفصال فيما بين الأجسام، وأن دعوى إمكان الاتصال فيما بين كل جسمين حتى الفلك والعنصر بحسب الطبيعة الجسمية ربما لا عدل إلى طريق الانفعال فقال : وبالجملة لا يمكن أن يحصل الاختلافات المقدارية والشكلية عن فاعلها في الامتداد، إلا بعد أن يكون فيه قوة الانفصال المقتضية للمادة.
And to alert to this meaning, they introduced connection along with separation; otherwise, the connection of bodies with one another has no role in the difference of their shapes and magnitudes. And upon this is interpreted what was said in Sharh al-Isharat: that the difference between bodies is not conceivable except by the separation of some from others and the connection of some with others, and that necessitates matter. And when the trace of weakness appeared on this path—based on the fact that they founded the existence of matter on the possibility of connection and separation in the body itself, even if it existed only such that there is no connection and separation between bodies, and that the claim of the possibility of connection between every two bodies, even the celestial sphere and the element, according to corporeal nature—perhaps he turned to the path of passion (infial) and said: In summary, it is not possible for the differences in magnitude and shape to occur from their agent in extension unless there is in it the potential for separation (qawwat al-infisal), which necessitates matter.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of the proof for the existence of matter (al-madda) in bodies. The author explains that earlier thinkers introduced the notion of connection (ittisal) and separation (infisal) to indicate that bodies must have a substrate allowing for changes. He quotes Sharh al-Isharat to show that difference between bodies is only conceivable through these relations, necessitating matter. However, this approach is weak because it assumes connection and separation within the body itself, whereas the real issue is between bodies. So the argument shifts to a different path: the path of passion (infial), meaning the body must have a potential to receive separation, implying matter. This demonstrates the necessity of matter as a substrate for change in size and shape.

Key Terms

اتصال
انفصال
قوة الانفصال
انفعال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from connection and separation between bodies is a sufficient proof for the existence of matter.
Correction: The author shows it is weak because it relies on connection/separation within the same body, not between bodies. The corrected proof focuses on the internal potential for separation (passion).

Checkpoint
Why was the first argument from connection and separation considered weak?
A) Because it relied on a principle not accepted by opponents.
B) Because it based the existence of matter on connection and separation within the body itself, not between bodies.
C) Because it was circular.
D) Because it assumed matter exists without proof.

Key Takeaway
Matter as a substrate is necessary for bodies to have the potential for separation and change in shape and magnitude.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The argument in this lesson critiques a weak proof and moves to a stronger one. In your own life, have you ever relied on weak proofs for your faith, only to later find their insufficiency? Consider one doctrine you hold—how firm is its foundation? Will you now examine the foundations of your beliefs with the same scrutiny the author applied to the connection/separation argument?

Your very existence is a continuous process of separation and recombination. What does that fragility and constant dependence teach you about your Creator's sustaining power? How will you now view your body differently—not as a possession but as a constantly renewed sign of Allah's power?

First Answer: Matter as Receptor for Difference in Whole and Part
First answer: matter as receptor explains difference in whole and part

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first answer to the objection about whole and part, which explains how matter as a receptor accounts for differences in shape and magnitude.

Core Idea
Matter's receptivity to connection and separation explains why whole and part differ.

In this passage, the author responds to an objection against the argument for matter by providing two distinct answers. The first answer emphasizes that matter inherently accepts both wholeness and partiality through connection and separation, so the difference in shape and magnitude between a whole and its part is due to the receptor (matter) rather than the agent (form). This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the first answer passage
Read the Arabic passage for the first answer carefully, then its translation. Note the key claim: matter accepts connection and separation.
The passage contrasts matter with corporeal form abstracted from matter.
2
Identify the key concepts
Identify 'matter' (مادة), 'wholeness' (كلية), 'partiality' (جزئية), 'connection' (اتصال), 'separation' (انفصال), 'shape' (شكل), 'magnitude' (مقدار).
3
Understand the contrast with abstract form
Notice that the author contrasts matter with corporeal form abstracted from matter. In the latter, the same difference is inconceivable.
This contrast supports the necessity of matter.
4
Reflect on the argument's purpose
Ask yourself: how does this answer defend the existence of matter? It shows that without matter, the variation between whole and part cannot be explained.
This connects to the larger argument for matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب عن النقض أيضاً بوجهين : أحدهما: أن هناك مادة تقبل الكلية والجزئية لقبولها بذاتها الاتصال والانفصال، فيعود اختلاف الشكل والمقدار فيما بين الكل والجزء إلى اختلاف القابل، وإن كان الفاعل واحداً هو الصورة النوعية ، بخلاف الصورة الجسمية . إذا فرضناها مجردة عن المادة، فإنه لا يتصور فيها ذلك، لأن حصول الجزئية بالانقسام، والكلية بالالتئام من لواحق المادة . وثانيهما: أن هناك مانعاً هو الجزئية، فإنه لما حصل للكل ذلك الشكل والمقدار امتنع بالضرورة حصوله للجزء، ما دام الجزء جزءاً ، والكل كلا ، ولا يتصور ذلك في الصورة المجردة من المادة وهذا عائد إلى الأول، إلا أنه يرد عليه أن الجزء وإن امتنع كونه على مقدار الكل، لا يمتنع كونه على شكله كتدوير الفلك وحامله، والمقصود بالنقض هو الشكل، وإنما المقدار استطراد. وجوابه أن الجزئية تمنع لزوم كون الجزء شكل الكل ضرورة امتناع كونه أجزاء الكرة ، وهذا كاف في دفع النقض، على أن مقتضى عدم التعدد في الفاعل والقابل هو أن يكون شكل الجزء والكل واحداً بالشخص، ولا خفاء في أن الجزئية تمنع ذلك.
And the objection was also answered in two ways: First: that there is matter that accepts both wholeness and partiality because it inherently accepts connection and separation, so the difference in shape and magnitude between the whole and the part reduces to a difference in the receptor, even if the agent is one, namely the specific form. This is unlike the corporeal form if we suppose it abstracted from matter, for it is not conceivable in it, because the attainment of partiality through division and entirety through cohesion are accidents of matter. Second: that there is a prevention (mani'), namely partiality (juziyya), for when the whole acquires that shape and magnitude, it is necessarily impossible for the part to acquire it as long as the part is a part and the whole is a whole. And this is not conceivable in the form abstracted from matter. But this returns to the first, except that it is objected that although the part cannot have the magnitude of the whole, it is not impossible for it to have its shape, like the rotation of the celestial sphere and its carrier; but the intended objection is about shape, while magnitude is incidental. And the answer is that partiality prevents the necessity of the part having the shape of the whole, due to the impossibility of it being parts of the sphere, and this suffices to repel the objection. Moreover, the requirement of the lack of multiplicity in agent and receptor is that the shape of the part and whole be identical in individuality, and it is not hidden that partiality prevents that.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents two responses to an objection against the argument for matter. The objection likely questioned how a single body (whole) and its parts can have different shapes and sizes if matter is not involved. First answer: matter (madda) inherently accepts both wholeness and partiality through connection and separation, so differences in shape/magnitude between whole and part stem from the receptor (matter), not the agent (form). Second answer: partiality (juziyya) itself prevents the part from having the same shape as the whole once the whole is formed. An objection is raised: a part might still have the same shape as the whole (like a sphere and its arc?). The response: partiality prevents identity of shape because a part cannot be all parts of the sphere. This shows that without matter, such differentiation is impossible.

A) The form.
B) The agent.
C) The receptor (matter).
D) The prevention.

Key Takeaway
Matter's inherent receptivity to connection and separation explains why whole and part differ in shape and magnitude.

?
The passage teaches that the receptor explains difference, not just the agent. When you fall into sin, do you blame external factors (the agent) more than your own inner state that accepted the sin? Second Answer: Prevention of Partiality
Second answer: partiality as prevention, with objection and reply

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second answer to the objection, which introduces the concept of 'prevention' (mani') in the form of partiality (juziyya), and how it overcomes a further objection.

Core Idea
Partiality itself prevents the part from having the whole's shape, even if magnitude is excluded.

The second answer introduces a direct prevention: partiality itself makes it impossible for a part to acquire the shape and magnitude of the whole. However, an objection is raised that a part could still have the same shape as the whole (e.g., a spherical part). The author responds by showing that partiality prevents identity of shape because a part cannot be all parts of the sphere. This lesson examines this subtle argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the second answer passage
Read the Arabic passage for the second answer including the objection and reply. Understand the concept of 'prevention' (مانع).
The passage includes an objection and its answer.
2
Understand the main claim
The author states that partiality (جزئية) itself prevents the part from having the whole's shape and magnitude.
This is a direct prevention, not a difference in receptor.
3
Analyze the objection
The objector argues that a part might still have the same shape as the whole even if not the same magnitude. Example: a spherical part of a sphere?
The objection focuses on shape, not magnitude.
4
Examine the reply
The reply: partiality prevents identity of shape because a part cannot be all parts of the sphere simultaneously.
This refutes the objection by showing impossibility.
5
Connect to the larger argument
Reflect on how this answer supports the necessity of matter: without matter, such prevention would not exist.

The Non-Causal Concomitance Between Hyle and Form
The author refutes causal models for hyle-form concomitance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why hyle and form are mutually dependent without one being the cause of the other, and the key arguments against causal relations between them.

Core Idea
Hyle and form rely on each other, but neither causes the other.

In this passage, the author takes up the fourth point concerning the relationship between prime matter (hyle) and form. He clarifies that their necessary concomitance does not arise from one causing the other, but from each needing the other for its own persistence. This sets the stage for rejecting causal models and highlighting the unique nature of their attachment.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the thesis
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that the author's central claim is that the mutual dependency between hyle and form is not due to causality.
The phrase 'ليس لعلية أحديهما' is key.
2
Understand why hyle needs form and form needs hyle
The author explains that hyle needs some form to endure, and can persist through successive forms. Form, on the other hand, needs a specific hyle for individuation. Consider the analogy of a ceiling with supports.
The celestial sphere is an exception where a single form is permanent due to external causes.
3
Examine the arguments against causality in both directions
The author provides reasons why form is not the cause of hyle and why hyle is not the cause of form. Focus on the specific arguments: form inheres in hyle, form can change while hyle remains, etc. For hyle: a receptor cannot be an agent, hyle needs form for actual existence, etc.
This part uses technical terms like 'قابل' and 'فاعل'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الرابع) إن التلازم بينهما ليس لعلية أحديهما ، بل لاحتياج الهيولي في بقائها إلى صورة ما، والصورة في تشخصها إلى هيولي بعينها . قد ثبت امتناع كل من الهيولي والصورة بدون الأخرى ، فاحتيج إلى بيان ذلك على وجه لا يدور، وذلك أن الهيولي تحتاج في بقائها إلى صورة لا بعينها، وتبقى محفوظة بصور متواردة كالسقف يبقى بدعائم تزال واحدة وتقام أخرى . نعم قد يلزم صورة واحدة لأسباب خارجية كما في الفلك، والصورة أن تحتاج في تشخصها إلى الهيولي المعينة التي هي محليها لما علم من شكلها إنما يكون بالمادة وما يتبعها من العوارض وليست الصورة علة للهيولي لكونها حالة فيها محتاجة إليها، ولكونها مقارنة لما هو متأخر عن الهيولي. أعني التناهي والتشكل التابعين للمادة، ولكونها جائزة الزوال إلى صورة أخرى، مع بقاء الهيولي بعينها، ولا يعقل في الشيء المعين أن تكون علته شيئاً لا بعينه، وليست الهيولي علة للصورة لما تقرر عندهم من أن القابل لا يكون فاعلاً، ومن أن الهيولي لا تقوم بالفعل إلا بالصورة، فتكون محتاجة إليها في الوجود متأخرة عنها ، ولأنها قابلة لصور غير متناهية، فلا تكون علة لشيء منها لعدم الأولوية، وإن انضم إليها ما يفيد الأولوية لم يكن للهيولي إلا القبول، والحق أن بيان كيفية تعلق الهيولي بالصورة، وامتناع علية إحداهما
He said (the fourth): The concomitance between them is not due to the causality of one of them, but rather due to the need of hyle (hayula) in its subsistence for some form, and the form in its individuation for a specific hyle. It has been established that each of hyle and form is impossible without the other, so it is necessary to demonstrate that in a way that does not involve circularity. And that is that hyle in its subsistence needs a form not specifically determined, and it remains preserved by successive forms, like a ceiling that remains with supports, one being removed and another being set up. Yes, a single form may be necessitated by external causes, as in the celestial sphere. And the form needs in its individuation the specific hyle that is its locus, because it is known that its shape is only through matter and its attendant accidents. And the form is not a cause of hyle because it inheres in it and is in need of it, and because it is concomitant with what is posterior to hyle, namely finitude and shaping which are consequent upon matter, and because it is liable to pass away into another form while the hyle remains the same. And it is not intelligible for a specific thing that its cause be something not specific. And hyle is not a cause of form because it is established among them that the receptor cannot be an agent, and because hyle does not subsist actually except by form, so it is in need of it in existence and is posterior to it, and because it is receptive to infinite forms, so it cannot be a cause of any of them due to lack of priority. Even if something that gives priority is attached to it, hyle has only receptivity. And the truth is that the explanation of how hyle is attached to form and the impossibility of the causality of either...
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth point of the discussion, which concerns the relationship between hyle (prime matter) and form. The author states that the mutual dependency (talazum) between them is not due to one being the cause of the other, but because each requires the other for its own subsistence. Hyle needs some form to endure, and it can persist through successive forms (like a ceiling needing supports). However, in special cases like the celestial sphere, a single form may be permanent due to external causes. Form, in turn, needs a specific hyle to be individuated, as its shape depends on matter. The author then argues that form is not the cause of hyle because form inheres in hyle and depends on it, and because form can change while hyle remains. Hyle is not the cause of form because a receptor cannot be an agent, hyle needs form for actual existence, and hyle is receptive to infinite forms, so it cannot be the cause of any particular form. The passage concludes that the true explanation lies in understanding their attachment without assuming causality.

تشخص
علة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If two things always coexist, one must be the cause of the other.
Correction: The passage shows that hyle and form necessarily coexist, but the author provides multiple arguments to prove that neither is the cause of the other; their relationship is one of mutual need, not causation.

Checkpoint
Why is hyle not a cause of form according to the fourth point?
A) Because hyle is infinite.
B) Because hyle is a receptor and cannot be an agent.
C) Because form is eternal.
D) Because hyle is specific.

Key Takeaway
Hyle and form are necessarily concomitant, but neither is the cause of the other; their relationship is based on mutual need, not causal production.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The hyle is not a cause because it merely receives; it does not act. Are there areas in your life where you have been a passive receiver but erroneously thought you were the agent? How can you accept your dependency without claiming causality?
?
Form individuates only through specific matter. Consider your own identity: what specific circumstances or relationships have shaped who you are? Do you wrongly think they are the cause of your existence rather than just necessary conditions for your individuation?

Answer: Partiality Prevents Part-Whole Form Identity
Refutes objection by showing partiality prevents part-whole form identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how partiality (juz'iyya) prevents the form of a part from being identical to the form of the whole, and why this effectively refutes the objection.

Core Idea
Partiality inherently prevents a part from having the same form as the whole.

This passage addresses a key objection in causal reasoning: if the agent and recipient are one, shouldn't the part and whole share the same form? The author explains that partiality itself blocks this identity, using the concrete example of a sphere to illustrate why a part cannot constitute the whole's form.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage closely
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice that the answer is presented in two parts: a direct rejection and an additional implication.
2
Identify the main claim
The core claim is that partiality (al-juz'iyya) prevents the necessity of the part being the form of the whole. Find the phrase that states this and understand the reasoning that follows.
The reason given is 'due to the impossibility of it being parts of the sphere.'
3
Understand the sphere example
Consider why a part of a sphere cannot be the sphere's form. This shows the impossibility. Then note the second part about identity in person and how partiality prevents even that.
This connects to the broader context of causality and form.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجوابه أن الجزئية تمنع لزوم كون الجزء شكل الكل ضرورة امتناع كونه أجزاء الكرة ، وهذا كاف في دفع النقض، على أن مقتضى عدم التعدد في الفاعل والقابل هو أن يكون شكل الجزء والكل واحداً بالشخص، ولا خفاء في جميع أن الجزئية تمنع ذلك
And its answer is that partiality prevents the necessity of the part being the form of the whole, due to the impossibility of it being parts of the sphere. This is sufficient to rebut the objection. Moreover, the implication of the absence of multiplicity in agent and recipient is that the form of the part and the whole is identical in person, and it is not hidden in all [cases] that partiality prevents that.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents an answer to an objection concerning the relationship between the form of a part and the form of the whole. The argument is that partiality (al-juz'iyya) prevents the part from having the same form as the whole, using the example of a sphere: a part cannot be parts of the sphere (i.e., it cannot be the sphere itself). It then argues that if there is no multiplicity in agent and recipient, the form of part and whole would be identical in person (شخص), but partiality prevents that, so the objection is refuted.

Key Terms
جزئية

The Mutual Dependence of Hyle and Form: Not Causal
Fourth argument: hyle-form dependency is not causal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the mutual dependence of hyle and form is not due to one being the cause of the other, but rather due to their distinct needs for persistence and individuation.

Core Idea
Hyle and form depend on each other by need, not causation.

This passage presents the fourth argument about the relationship between hyle (prime matter) and form. It establishes that their necessary connection is not causal but stems from each one's specific requirement: hyle needs form for its persistence, and form needs a specific hyle for its individuation. Let's explore the reasoning in the original text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get an overview of the argument. Pay attention to the main claim: the connection is not causal.
The passage uses technical terms; don't worry about every detail yet.
2
Identify the two key claims
Notice that the passage argues both that form is not the cause of hyle and that hyle is not the cause of form. Underline the sentences that state these.
Look for 'ليس الصورة علة للهيولي' and 'ليست الهيولي علة للصورة'.
3
Analyze the reasons against form causing hyle
The passage lists several reasons why form cannot be the cause of hyle. List them: form inheres in hyle, is simultaneous with effects posterior to hyle, and can be removed while hyle persists.
These reasons rely on the nature of causation and inherence.
4
Analyze the reasons against hyle causing form
Similarly, examine why hyle cannot be the cause of form: the recipient cannot be an agent, hyle needs form to subsist, and hyle is receptive of infinite forms without priority.
The principle 'al-qabilu la yakunu fa'ilan' is invoked.
5
Reflect on the concluding remark
The passage ends by noting the difficulty of fully explicating this issue, acknowledging the efforts of later scholars. Consider why this topic is challenging.
This humility underscores the depth of the problem.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الرابع) إن التلازم بينهما (۱) ليس لعلية أحديهما ، بل لاحتياج الهيولي في بقائها إلى صورة ما، والصورة في تشخصها إلى هيولي بعينها . قد ثبت امتناع كل من الهيولي والصورة بدون الأخرى (٢) ، فاحتيج إلى بيان ذلك على وجه لا يدور، وذلك أن الهيولي تحتاج في بقائها إلى صورة لا بعينها، وتبقى محفوظة بصور متواردة كالسقف يبقى بدعائم تزال واحدة وتقام أخرى (٣) . نعم قد يلزم صورة واحدة لأسباب خارجية كما في الفلك، والصورة تحتاج في تشخصها إلى الهيولي المعينة التي هي محليها لما علم من شكلها إنما يكون بالمادة وما يتبعها من العوارض وليست الصورة علة للهيولي لكونها حالة فيها محتاجة إليها، ولكونها مقارنة لما هو متأخر عن الهيولي. أعني التناهي والتشكل التابعين للمادة، ولكونها جائزة الزوال إلى صورة أخرى، مع بقاء الهيولي بعينها، ولا يعقل في الشيء المعين أن تكون علته شيئاً لا بعينه، وليست الهيولي علة للصورة لما تقرر عندهم من أن القابل لا يكون فاعلاً، ومن أن الهيولي لا تقوم بالفعل إلا بالصورة، فتكون محتاجة إليها في الوجود متأخرة عنها ، ولأنها قابلة لصور غير متناهية، فلا تكون علة لشيء منها لعدم الأولوية، وإن انضم إليها (٤) ما يفيد الأولوية لم يكن للهيولي إلا القبول، والحق أن بيان كيفية تعلق الهيولي بالصورة، وامتناع علية إحداهما للأخرى، ووجوب تقدم الصورة على الهيولي من حيث هي صورة ما، وتأخرها عنها من حيث هي صورة مشخصة على وجه يندفع عنه ما سنح عليه (۱) من الإشكالات عسر جداً، والمتأخرون قد بذلوا فيه المجهود، وبلغوا مداه، ولو علمنا فيه خيراً لأوردناه
The fourth said: The necessary connection between them is not due to one being the cause of the other, but rather due to the need of hyle for its persistence to some form, and the need of form for its individuation to a specific hyle. It has been established that each of hyle and form is impossible without the other, so there is need for an explanation of this without circularity. That is because hyle, in its persistence, needs a form not specifically determined, and it remains preserved by successive forms, like a roof that remains by supports that are removed one and replaced by another. Indeed, a single form may become necessary due to external causes, as in the celestial sphere. And form, in its individuation, needs the specific hyle that is its locus, because it is known that its shape is only through matter and its accidental concomitants. And form is not a cause of hyle because it inheres in it and is in need of it, and because it is simultaneous with what is posterior to hyle, namely finitude and shape that follow matter, and because it is subject to removal to another form while the hyle itself remains. And it is not intelligible for a specific thing that its cause should be something not specifically determined. Nor is hyle a cause of form because of what is established among them that the recipient is not an agent, and because hyle does not subsist in act except through form, so it is in need of it and posterior to it in existence, and because it is receptive of infinite forms, so it cannot be a cause of any one of them for lack of priority. Even if something is added to it that gives priority, hyle has only receptivity. The truth is that explaining the mode of attachment of hyle to form, the impossibility of one being the cause of the other, the necessity of form being prior to hyle insofar as it is some form, and its being posterior to it insofar as it is an individuating form, in a way that repels the objections that have been advanced against it, is very difficult. The later scholars have exerted great effort in it and reached its limit. If we knew any good in it, we would have presented it.
Source pages p. 77

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument (al-rabi') regarding the relationship between hyle (prime matter) and form. It begins with a statement: their mutual connection is not due to one being the cause of the other, but because hyle needs form for its persistence (but not a specific form), and form needs a specific hyle for its individuation. The explanatory section then argues that hyle persists through successive forms, akin to a roof supported by replaceable pillars, except in special cases like the celestial sphere. Form, on the other hand, requires a specific hyle as its locus because its shape depends on matter. The explanation then lists reasons why form is not the cause of hyle: form inheres in hyle and is simultaneous with properties posterior to hyle (finitude and shape), and form can be removed while hyle remains. Similarly, hyle is not the cause of form because the recipient cannot be an agent, hyle needs form to subsist in act, and hyle is receptive of infinite forms with no priority. The passage concludes by noting the difficulty of fully explicating this relationship, acknowledging the efforts of later scholars.

Key Terms

صورة
تلازم
علة
تشخص
قبول
فعل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the mutual dependence of hyle and form implies causation between them.
Correction: This passage clarifies it is not causation but distinct needs: hyle needs form for persistence, form needs hyle for individuation.

Checkpoint
According to the fourth argument, why is form not a cause of hyle?
A) Because form is prior to hyle.
B) Because form inheres in hyle and is simultaneous with what is posterior to hyle.
C) Because hyle is the cause of form.
D) Because form is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The mutual dependence of hyle and form is a result of need (hyle for persistence, form for individuation), not a causal relationship.

Proof of Substantial Forms as Principles of Species Diversity
This passage proves substantial forms as principles of species diversity, not accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the differences in bodies' effects cannot be explained by accidents alone and necessitate substantial forms as principles of diversity.

Core Idea
Differences in bodies demand substantial forms, not accidents, to avoid infinite regress.

This passage presents the fifth argument from the doctrine of hyle and form, establishing substantial forms as the principles of species diversity. The author argues that observable differences in bodies' effects—such as water's ease of separation versus earth's difficulty—cannot be due to shared corporeality or accidents, because that would lead to an infinite regress. Instead, these differences must stem from substantial forms that constitute the very essence of each species.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument and its objection
Read the Arabic passage once through. Then read the English translation. Pay attention to the objection raised and how the author responds.
The argument is compact; take your time to follow the logical flow.
2
Identify the key reasoning
Identify why the author argues that the principles of diversity must be forms, not accidents. Notice the appeal to infinite regress and the impossibility of a substance subsisting by an accident.
The objection tries to undermine the argument, but the author turns it back on the objector.
3
Connect to the broader doctrine
Relate this argument to the doctrine of hyle and form. Understand that substantial forms are constituents of substances, explaining why water and fire have different natures despite sharing matter and corporeal form.
This argument is one of the branches of the hyle and form theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الخامس) إن اختلاف الأجسام في الآثار ليست للجسمية المشتركة، بل لأمر مختص غير عارض دفعاً للتسلسل، وهو الصورة النوعية، ونوقض باختلاف الصورة، فإن التزم بقاءها لا إلى نهاية أو استناد اختلافها إلى اختلاف الاستعدادات التزمنا مثله في العوارض. وقد يقال إن مبادىء آثار الأجسام أمور بها تنوعها وتحصلها، فلا يكون إلا جوهر مقومه، وحاصله أنا نقطع باختلاف حقيقتي الماء والنار مثلاً، مع الاشتراك في المادة والصورة الجسمية، فلا بد من الاختلاف بمقوم جوهري نسميه الصورة النوعية، ومبناه على امتناع تقوم الجسم بعرض قائم بجزئيه )، أو بجوهر غير حال في مادته . من تفاريع القول بالهيولي والصورة إثبات صور نوعية، هي مبادىء اختلاف الأنواع بالآثار، وبيانه أنه لاخفاء في أن للأجسام آثاراً مختلفة (۳) كقبول الانفكاك والالتئام بسهولة كما في الماء أو بعسر كما في الأرض، أو امتناع عن ذلك كما في الفلك، وكالاختصاص بما لها بحسب طبعها من الأشكال والأمكنة والأوضاع ، وليس ذلك بمجرد الجسمية المشتركة ولا الهيولي القابلة وهو ظاهر ولا بأمر مفارق لتساوي نسبته إلى جميع الأجسام، ولأن الكلام في آثار الأجسام فيلزم الخلف، فتعين أن تكون بأمور مختصة مقارنة ، ويجب أن تكون صوراً لا أعراضاً، لأنا ننقل الكلام إلى أسباب تلك الأعراض فيتسلسل، ولأن تنوع الأجسام وتحصلها موقوف على الاتصاف بتلك الأمور، ومن المحال تقوم الجوهر بالعرض
The fifth said: The difference of bodies in their effects is not due to the common corporeality, but to a specific matter that is not an accident, to avoid infinite regress, and that is the substantial form. It was objected by the difference of form, for if one posits its persistence ad infinitum or ascribes its difference to difference of dispositions, we would posit the same for accidents. And it is said that the principles of effects of bodies are things by which they are diversified and actualized, so they can only be a constituent substance. Its summary is that we are certain of the difference of the realities of water and fire, for example, despite their sharing in matter and corporeal form, so there must be difference by a substantial constituent we call substantial form, and it is based on the impossibility of the body's subsistence by an accident subsisting in its parts, or by a substance not inhering in its matter. From the branches of the doctrine of hyle and form is the establishment of substantial forms, which are the principles of the diversity of species in their effects. Its explanation: it is not hidden that bodies have different effects, like acceptance of separation and reunion easily as in water, or with difficulty as in earth, or impossibility as in the celestial sphere, and like their specific shapes, places, and positions according to their nature. This is not due to common corporeality nor to receptive hyle, as is clear, nor to a separate thing because of its equal relation to all bodies, and because the discussion is about the effects of bodies, so that would be a circular argument. So it must be due to specific concomitant things, and they must be forms, not accidents, because we would then transfer the discussion to the causes of those accidents and an infinite regress would follow, and because the diversification and actualization of bodies is dependent on being qualified by those things, and it is impossible for a substance to subsist by an accident.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage presents the fifth argument (al-khamis) establishing substantial forms as principles of diversity in bodies. It begins with a statement: differences in bodies' effects (e.g., water vs. fire vs. celestial sphere) are not due to common corporeality but to a specific non-accidental principle – the substantial form (al-sura al-naw'iyya). It addresses an objection: if someone argues that the form itself could differ, leading to infinite regress, the same would apply to accidents. The argument then asserts that the principles of effects must be constituent substances, not accidents, and gives the example of water and fire. The explanatory section elaborates: observable differences in effects (ease of separation, shape, etc.) cannot be due to shared corporeality, receptive hyle, or a separate entity, but must be due to specific concomitant forms, because accidents would lead to infinite regress and a substance cannot subsist by an accident. Thus, substantial forms are necessary to explain real diversity.

Key Terms
صورة نوعية
جسم
عرض
جوهر
نوع
أثر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That differences in bodies' effects can be explained by accidents alone.
Correction: The argument shows that explaining diversity by accidents leads to infinite regress when asking for the causes of those accidents. Therefore, the principles must be substantial forms, which are constituents of the substance itself.

Checkpoint
According to the fifth argument, why must the principles of diversity in bodies be forms rather than accidents?
A) Because accidents are not real.
B) Because if they were accidents, we would have an infinite regress when asking for their causes.
C) Because forms are more noble than accidents.
D) Because bodies cannot have accidents.

Key Takeaway
Substantial forms are necessary to explain the real diversity of bodies without falling into infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The argument shows that an infinite regress of causes is impossible. In your own life, are there patterns of excuse-making or blame-shifting where you create an infinite chain, avoiding final accountability to Allah? What would it mean for you to cut that chain and accept that your existence rests on Him alone?
?
If a substance cannot be constituted by an accident, what does that imply about your own identity? Are you building your life on temporary accidents—status, wealth, opinions—or on the substantial reality of your soul before Allah? What one change can you make today to ground yourself in what is lasting?

Substantial Forms as the Source of Differences
Quotation of al-Maqasid's fifth point with objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Muslim theologians posited substantial forms (الصورة النوعية) as the source of differences among bodies, and how they defended this against the objection of infinite regress.

Core Idea
Substantial forms explain why bodies differ in their effects, without falling into infinite regress.

This passage introduces the fifth point of al-Maqasid, addressing a key question in natural philosophy: why do bodies of different kinds (e.g., water and fire) produce different effects? The author argues that the difference cannot be due to common corporeality or accidental properties, but must be a constitutive substance—the substantial form. He then considers an objection and provides a response, grounding the argument in the impossibility of infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice the three-part structure: statement, objection, response.
2
Identify the core claim
Pinpoint the claim: differences in effects are due to substantial form, not common corporeality. Ask why common corporeality cannot account for differences.
The key phrase is 'بل لأمر مختص غير عارض دفعاً للتسلسل'.
3
Understand the objection
The objection says the same infinite regress could apply to forms themselves. How? If forms persist indefinitely or their differences depend on preparedness, then accidents would also need such explanation.
This is a standard kalām objection: applying the same argument to forms leads to an infinite regress.
4
Examine the response
The response asserts that principles of effects must be constitutive substances, not accidents or substances not inhering in matter. The basis is the impossibility of a body subsisting through an accident in its parts.
5
Reflect on the summary and basis
The passage ends with a concrete example (water and fire) and the logical basis: the impossibility of a body subsisting by an accident or by a non-inhering substance.
This reinforces that substantial form is a constitutive substance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ قال (الخامس) إن اختلاف الأجسام في الآثار ليست للجسمية المشتركة، بل لأمر مختص غير عارض دفعاً للتسلسل، وهو الصورة النوعية، ونوقض باختلاف الصورة، فإن التزم بقاءها لا إلى نهاية أو استناد اختلافها إلى اختلاف الاستعدادات التزمنا مثله في العوارض. وقد يقال إن مبادىء آثار الأجسام أمور بها تنوعها وتحصلها، فلا يكون إلا جوهر مقومه، وحاصله أنا نقطع باختلاف حقيقتي الماء والنار مثلاً، مع الاشتراك في المادة والصورة الجسمية، فلا بد من الاختلاف بمقوم جوهري نسميه الصورة النوعية، ومبناه على امتناع تقوم الجسم بعرض قائم بجزئيه، أو بجوهر غير حال في مادته ]
He said (the fifth): That the difference of bodies in their effects is not due to the common corporeality, but to a specific non-accidental thing, to prevent infinite regress, and that is the substantial form. And it was objected by the difference of the form: for if one commits to its persistence ad infinitum, or to the dependence of its difference on the difference of preparednesses, we would commit the like for accidents. And it may be said that the principles of the effects of bodies are things by which they are diversified and realized, so they cannot be anything but a constitutive substance. Its summary: we are certain of the difference of the reality of water and fire, for example, despite their sharing in matter and corporeal form, so there must be difference in a constitutive substance which we call the substantial form. And its basis is the impossibility of the subsistence of a body by an accident subsisting in its two parts, or by a substance not inhering in its matter.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a quotation of the fifth point from al-Maqasid, followed by a brief objection and a reply. The author begins by stating the fifth point: the differences among bodies in their effects are not due to the common corporeality but to something specific and non-accidental, which is the substantial form, to avoid an infinite regress. Then an objection is raised: the same infinite regress could apply to the forms themselves. If one says that forms persist indefinitely or their differences depend on differences in preparedness, then we would have to say the same for accidents. Then a response is given: the principles of the effects of bodies are things that diversify and realize them, so they must be a constitutive substance. The summary is that we are certain of the difference between the realities of water and fire despite their sharing matter and corporeal form, so there must be a constitutive difference, which is the substantial form. The basis is that a body cannot subsist by an accident that inheres in its parts, nor by a substance not in its matter.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument for substantial forms is based on circular reasoning about effects.
Correction: The passage shows it is to avoid infinite regress, by positing a constitutive substance that grounds differences.

Checkpoint

A) Differences in bodies are due to common corporeality
B) Differences are due to accidental forms
C) Differences are due to substantial forms

Key Takeaway
Substantial forms are necessary to explain differences in bodies' effects while avoiding infinite regress.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Proof for Substantial Forms from Diversity of Effects
Al-Sanusi presents a detailed proof for substantial forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational argument for substantial forms as the principles of specific differences in bodies, and why they cannot be accidents.

Core Idea
Substantial forms are necessary to explain why bodies have distinct, stable effects.

In this passage, the author builds a rigorous proof for substantial forms by examining the observable diversity of effects in bodies. He systematically eliminates alternative explanations, showing that only substantial forms can account for the differences without leading to circular reasoning or infinite regress. Pay close attention to the logical steps he uses to rule out accidents as the cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the logical connectors like 'and', 'because', 'so' that structure the argument.
The passage is tightly argued; read it at least twice.
2
Identify the three rejected alternatives
The author mentions three things that cannot explain diverse effects: common corporeality, receptive hyle, and a separate cause. Find each and understand why they are rejected.
These are classic positions in kalam.
3
Understand the positive conclusion
After eliminating the alternatives, the author concludes that the principles must be specific, concomitant things. Then he argues they must be forms, not accidents, using two arguments: infinite regress and the impossibility of substance subsisting by accident.
4
Review the guided breakdown
Use the guided breakdown below to systematically go through each step of the argument. This will help you internalize the logical flow.
5
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to check your grasp of why forms cannot be accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
من تفاريع القول بالهيولي والصورة إثبات صور نوعية، هي مبادىء اختلاف الأنواع بالآثار، وبيانه أنه لا خفاء في أن للأجسام آثاراً مختلفة كقبول الانفكاك والالتئام بسهولة كما في الماء أو بعسر كما في الأرض، أو امتناع عن ذلك كما في الفلك، وكالاختصاص بما لها بحسب طبعها من الأشكال والأمكنة والأوضاع، وليس ذلك بمجرد الجسمية المشتركة ولا الهيولي القابلة وهو ظاهر ولا بأمر مفارق لتساوي نسبته إلى جميع الأجسام، ولأن الكلام في آثار الأجسام فيلزم الخلف، فتعين أن تكون بأمور مختصة مقارنة، ويجب أن تكون صوراً لا أعراضاً، لأنا ننقل الكلام إلى أسباب تلك الأعراض فيتسلسل، ولأن تنوع الأجسام وتحصلها موقوف على الاتصاف بتلك الأمور، ومن المحال تقوم الجوهر بالعرض.
Among the ramifications of the doctrine of hyle and form is the affirmation of substantial forms, which are the principles of the difference of species in their effects. Its explanation: There is no obscurity that bodies have diverse effects, such as accepting separation and reunion easily as in water, or with difficulty as in earth, or being impossible of that as in the celestial sphere, and such as the specificity of their shapes, places, and positions according to their nature. And that is not due to the common corporeality alone, nor to the receptive hyle, which is evident, nor to a separate thing because of its equal relation to all bodies, and because the discussion is about the effects of bodies, so the consequence would be a circle. So it is determined that they are due to specific, concomitant things. And they must be forms, not accidents, because we would transfer the discussion to the causes of those accidents, leading to infinite regress, and because the diversification and realization of bodies is dependent on being qualified by those things, and it is impossible for a substance to subsist by an accident.
Source pages p. 78

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the proof for substantial forms. The author states that among the ramifications of the doctrine of hyle (prime matter) and form is the affirmation of substantial forms, which are the principles of the difference of species in their effects. He then explains: Bodies clearly have diverse effects—for example, water easily separates and reunites, earth does so with difficulty, and the celestial sphere does not at all. They also have specific shapes, places, and positions by nature. These effects are not due solely to common corporeality (shared by all bodies), nor to the receptive hyle (which is indifferent), nor to a separate cause (since that would be equally related to all bodies, leading to a circle). Therefore, they must be due to specific, concomitant things. These things must be forms, not accidents, because if they were accidents, we would have to ask about their causes, leading to an infinite regress. Moreover, the diversification and realization of bodies depend on being qualified by those things, and it is impossible for a substance (the body) to subsist by an accident (which is dependent on a substance).

Correction: The passage argues they cannot be accidents because that would lead to an infinite regress (tasalsul) in explaining accidents, and because a substance cannot subsist by an accident.

Checkpoint

A) Because accidents are separate from bodies

Key Takeaway
Substantial forms are necessary to explain the diverse effects of bodies, and they cannot be accidents because that would lead to infinite regress and violate the metaphysical principle that substances do not subsist by accidents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just seen a proof that bodies have intrinsic substantial forms that determine their effects. When you look at the world around you—the water you drink, the earth you walk on, the stars above—do you truly believe these are not just accidents but have deep, God-given natures? How does this belief affect your trust in the order of creation and your gratitude to the One who designed it?
?
The passage rejects accidents as the basis of effects because that would lead to an infinite regress. In your own spiritual life, do you ever settle for superficial explanations for your actions—'it's just my mood,' 'it's just my habit'—rather than seeking the deeper, substantial cause? What would it mean to root your character in a real, stable form rather than fleeting accidents?
?
The proof argues that separate causes (like direct divine intervention for every effect) would lead to circular reasoning. Does your understanding of God’s action in the world allow for secondary causes—the substantial forms of creatures—or do you expect God to do everything directly? How does affirming substantial forms change how you see your own responsibility and the role of natural means in your life?
